    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="519755316f71411680019e054800272e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'be358fa2-9cbf-4bfb-9809-b0cef48634ea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-5013-4187"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0001-5013-4187</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>State Key Laboratory of Advanced Electromagnetic Engineering and Technology, School of Electrical and Electronic Engineering, Huazhong University of Science and Technology, Wuhan, Hubei, 430074 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:minzhou0729@hust.edu.cn"><span>minzhou0729@hust.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:kjiang@hust.edu.cn"><span>kjiang@hust.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Jiang/Kai">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>

Sodium-Ion Batteries link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/aenm.202303015">https://doi.org/10.1002/aenm.202303015</a></div><div class="epub-section cited-by-count"><span>Citations: <a    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d15358800" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Although Prussian blue analogues are the promising candidate cathode materials for the Na-ion batteries for the grid storage due to 3D open-framework structure and large interstitial “A” sites, high content of defects in the crystals obtained in the conventional strategy severely impede Na<sup>+</sup> migration, leading to an unsatisfactory power density. Here a novel epitaxial nucleation-assisted controlled crystallization approach to eliminate the structural defects of NaFeHCF crystals is reported. Due to their limited lattice misfit of only 4.87% (&lt; 5%) between the graphene and NaFeHCF as well as the electronegativity of the functional groups (─COOH, ─OH, ─CH(O)CH─), GO can act as the nucleation and subsequent epitaxial growth platform of NaFeHCF, which results in a unique one-corner-cut cubic nano-crystals morphologies with much decreased contents of defects (0.08 per formula unit). This enhanced lattice regularity significantly enhanced the speedy diffusion of Na cations (by 5 times) in the nanocrystals, resulting in the unprecedented rate capability of 96.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultra-high rate of 9&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (39&nbsp;s, 23228&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>), which is far exceeding that of any previously reported PBA-based cathodes to the knowledge, authenticating its superiority as a candidate for high-power sodium-ion batteries for the reliable grid energy storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303015-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Grid storage is an essential component for the utilization of sustainable and clean energy sources. Compared to traditional grid storage technologies such as thermal storage and pumped hydro, rechargeable batteries offer many benefits, such as higher conversion efficiency, energy density, and more effective power management. Considering the intermittence of the renewable energy, batteries with high power density are necessary to provide the energy quickly (usually within several seconds) and improve the reliability of the grid in the renewable energy systems, especially during periods of high energy demand.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0001_R_d15358767e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0003_R_d15358767e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The demand for battery-based grid storage systems is expected to reach 4500&nbsp;GWh in 2050.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0004_R_d15358767e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Currently, rechargeable Na-ion batteries (SIBs) appear to be a decent alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) toward grid-scale electrical energy storage (EES) on account of the widespread availability and low-budget of Na resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0005_R_d15358767e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, compared with Li ions (0.76&nbsp;Å), the larger radius (1.02&nbsp;Å) and sluggish kinetics of Na ions lead to a huge reduction of available insertion sites and diffusion channels in Na-insertion cathodes compared to Li-insertion counterparts, which own a high specific power density of 150–315&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg.<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0006_R_d15358767e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Renew. Sustain. Energy Rev.">6</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0007_R_d15358767e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, a host framework with a larger void space for Na ions storage is in urgent need.</p>
         
         <p>Remarkably, Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) with 3D rigid framework, large interstitial “A” sites (≈4.6&nbsp;Å diameter), and ionic diffusion channels (3.2&nbsp;Å diameter in〈100〉direction), as well as abundant redox-active centers and strong structural stability, have been deemed to be exceedingly desirable cathode materials for accommodating the large-size Na<sup>+</sup> compared with another oxide-based cathode,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0008_R_d15358767e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0009_R_d15358767e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Rec.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> resulting in an impressive theoretical capacity (170&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) based on two-electron redox reaction. The general chemical formula of PBAs could denoted as A<sub>x</sub>M<sub>1</sub>[M<sub>2</sub>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1-y</sub>◻<sub>y</sub>·nH<sub>2</sub>O (A = Li, Na, K，etc.; M<sub>1</sub> and M<sub>2</sub> = Fe, Mn, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn, Ti, etc. where ◻ are [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] defects; 0&nbsp;≤ x ≤ 2, 0 ≤ y ≤ 1).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0010_R_d15358767e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0011_R_d15358767e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, massive [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] defects were usually contained in the NaFeHCF crystals obtained in the conventional precipitation method with uncontrollable crystallization rate, which further greatly decreased the lattice regularity. The distorted lattice not only makes the PBAs skeleton prone to collapse during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion-extraction process but also hinders the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> and blocks the e<sup>−</sup> transport along the ordered Fe-C≡N─Fe framework, inducing the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> to be restricted in the micro-regions of the crystal lattice and the raise of ohmic polarization of the electrode, thus gravely deteriorating their rate capability.</p>
         
         <p>To improve the regularity of the lattice of the NaFeHCF, researchers have tried controlling the nucleation and growth rate of crystals during preparation. One&nbsp;well-known approach is to employ Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> as the single iron-source precursor during synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0012_R_d15358767e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Res.">12</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0015_R_d15358767e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Guo et&nbsp;al. prepared high-quality Na<sub>0.61</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.94</sub>◻<sub>0.06</sub>·2.6H<sub>2</sub>O cubes (300–600&nbsp;nm) could deliver an extraordinary cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be noted that this process was conducted in the strong acid conditions, which carries the risk of releasing highly toxic HCN and produce very low yields. Another available means of obtaining low-defect PBAs crystals is classical citrate-assisted controlled crystallization process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0016_R_d15358767e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0022_R_d15358767e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And Yang et&nbsp;al. proposed defect-free Na<sub>1.85</sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.99</sub>◻<sub>0.01</sub>·1.9H<sub>2</sub>O cubes (≈600&nbsp;nm) could exhibit an outstanding ≈90% capacity retention over 200 cycles (100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0016_R_d15358767e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the slow and long crystallization processes usually result in a large particle size,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0023_R_d15358767e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which renders the long diffusion pathways for the Na cations in the redox reaction, leading to a compromised rate capability and power densities. It can be inferred that nano-PBAs crystals with low defects can deliver the power densities and thus are expected to be further exploited for commercial SIBs application in the EES. But the controllable preparation of high-quality nano-PBAs with improved lattice regularity still remains a great challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report a novel epitaxial nucleation and growth strategy to improve the lattice regularity of the NaFeHCF nano crystals for the cathode materials in sodium battery with high power densities. Intriguingly, GO with abundant negatively charged functional groups (─COOH, ─OH, ─CH(O)CH─) is conceived as the nucleation sites. In the meanwhile, the perfect match of the lattice can lead ordered growth of the NaFeHCF on the GO subsequently, effectively alleviating the defects within the crystals by 0.08 per formula unit. The more regulated lattice provided a short and rapid diffusional pathway (≈5 times enhanced) during the redox, effectively boosting the rate capability and exhibiting a superior power density (23&nbsp;228&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>), which even outperformed that of Li-ion counterparts in the EES. Our findings provide the new opportunities for the development of methods for the controllable crystallization as well as the practical sodium battery with high power densities.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303015-sec-0020-title">2 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The synthesis routes of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO are depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0001">1</a>. First, 2&nbsp;mmol Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> was dissolved in 10&nbsp;mL graphene dispersion (10 wt%) to form solution A, and 2&nbsp;mmol FeCl<sub>3</sub> and 10&nbsp;mmol C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> were dissolved in 10&nbsp;mL graphene dispersion (10 wt%) to form solution B, both of which were placed in ice H<sub>2</sub>O bath. The function of ice H<sub>2</sub>O bath is to slow down the reaction rate and prevent the complete formation of Prussian blue at this step. The aim is to ensure the high crystalline epitaxial growth of Prussian blue and the one-step reduction of GO to rGO under subsequent hydrothermal conditions. Subsequently, solution A was slowly added to solution B and magnetically stirred for 10&nbsp;min, then transferred to a 50&nbsp;mL stainless steel reactor, sealed, and moved to an oven. Afterward, the oven was heated to 120°C for 12&nbsp;h, and then naturally cooled to room temperature. Ultimately, the resulting precipitates were separated by centrifugation and washed three times with distilled water and anhydrous ethanol successively, and then dried in a vacuum at 100°C for 24&nbsp;h. The reaction chemical equation could be summarized as:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202303015-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303015-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2becfa16-c468-4902-a763-21ce1b6e4f02/aenm202303015-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2becfa16-c468-4902-a763-21ce1b6e4f02/aenm202303015-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6fbb072d-e8a3-4591-b44e-6093a4eae6be/aenm202303015-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2becfa16-c468-4902-a763-21ce1b6e4f02/aenm202303015-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303015-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303015" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO synthesis. a) The conventional coprecipitation method of NaFeHCF. b) The nucleation-assisted controlled crystallization process of NaFeHCF@rGO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The samples with and without graphene in solution were labeled NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO, respectively.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0040-title">2.2 Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were performed on the Panalytical Xpert X-ray diffraction spectrometer (Holland) with Cu Kα radiation. Raman spectra were recorded on the LabRAM HR800 (France) using an Ar+ laser with a wavelength of 532&nbsp;nm. Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were collected on the VERTEX 70 FT-IR spectrometer (Germany) with KBr pellets. Thermogravimetric (TG) curves were measured on the FTA 449 F5 TG/DTA analyzer (Germany) from 30 to 500°C at a rate of 10°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> in N<sub>2</sub>. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses were measured by the AXIS-ULTRA DLD-600&nbsp;W (Japan) X-Ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy. The metal element was determined by the inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES, Agilent 700 Series, Germany). The organic element was determined by the elemental analyzer (Vario Micro cube, Germany). Morphology observations were carried out on the Sirion 200 field-emission scanning electron microscope (SEM, Germany). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observations were conducted on the Talos F200X (Holland) transmission electron microscope.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The working electrodes were prepared by coating the slurry of active material (NaFeHCF or NaFeHCF@rGO), conductive agent (Ketjen Black), and binder (PVDF) with a mass ratio of 7: 2: 1 on the aluminum carbon foil. The mass loading of active material was 1–2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for each electrode. The prepared working electrode, glass fiber filters as diaphragm, metal sodium plate as counter and reference electrodes, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>&nbsp;NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC) with 10 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) solution as electrolyte were assembled into 2025 type coin cell. The assembly process was carried out in the Ar-filled glove box with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> content below 0.1&nbsp;ppm (Ar% &gt; 99.99%, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1&nbsp;ppm, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 1&nbsp;ppm). Cyclic voltammetry measurements were performed on the CHI660C electrochemistry workstation from 2.0 to 4.2&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were recorded on the battery system (LAND cycler, Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co., China) from 2.0 to 4.2&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303015-sec-0060-title">3 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0070-title">3.1 Preparation of Low-Defects NaFeHCF through Epitaxial Nucleation and Growth</h3>
            
            <p>Commonly, the conventional method for preparing Na<sub>x</sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>y</sub>◻<sub>1-y</sub>·nH<sub>2</sub>O is based on the direct precipitation reaction of M<sup>2+</sup> cations and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> anions in aqueous solution,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0001">1a</a>. Its nucleation and growth processes occur instantaneously,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0016_R_d15358767e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> owing to the solubility product constant (K<sub>sp</sub>) of Na<sub>x</sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>y</sub> is minuscule, such as K<sub>sp</sub> = 3.3×10<sup>−41</sup> of NaFeHCF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0024_R_d15358767e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Manuf. Process.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It usually results in the formation of nano NaFeHCF particles with extremely poor crystallinity and extensive Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to obtain perfect crystal lattices with less defects, it is envisaged to introduce nucleation sites and guide the subsequent crystallization during synthesis. We choose GO as the nucleation initialing and crystallization guiding substrate for the epitaxial nucleation and growth of highly crystalline NaFeHCF nanoparticles, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0001">1b</a>. On the one hand, the GO possesses abundant functional groups of carboxyl, hydroxyl, and epoxy groups (─COOH, ─OH, ─CH(O)CH─), which can act as the nucleation sites due to the strong electrostatic interaction between these negatively charged functional groups with the positively charged Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0025_R_d15358767e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0026_R_d15358767e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the atomic arrangements of (111) plane in the NaFeHCF almost overlapped with that of the basal plane of the GO, indicating a limited mismatch of only 4.87% (&lt; 5%), a more specific explanation can be found in the supporting information (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This similar structure can guide the subsequent crystallization of the NaFeHCF and effectively alleviate the internal defects following the way in the semiconductor industry.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0027_R_d15358767e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Lett.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4−</sup> and the remaining Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions will crystallize along the GO layer, enabling the uniform and ordered growth of the NaFeHCF crystal with low-defect content.</p>
            
            <p>The SEM image in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information) reveals that the bare NaFeHCF crystals appear as reasonably dispersed irregular spherical nanoparticles with average sizes of less than 200&nbsp;nm. For the NaFeHCF@rGO samples in great contrast, the NaFeHCF particles are evenly scattered and anchored on the conductive rGO films (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0002">2</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information), which could realize the close contact between NaFeHCF and rGO, ascribing to the well atomic match at the interface between the NaFeHCF and rGO, which can further enhance the interfacial charge transfer. In addition, it is observed that the average particle size of NaFeHCF with GO is reduced compared to that of bare NaFeHCF, attributed to the role of GO acting as the dispersant to inhibit the collision between nanoparticles with high specific surface energy. Undoubtedly, the smaller and dispersed nanoparticles could shorten the Na<sup>+</sup> transport path and benefit the penetration of the electrolyte, thus facilitating the rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> cations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0015_R_d15358767e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0002">2a</a> shows a typical micron cubic morphology of NaFeHCF crystal, which is consistent with the synthesis conditions of NaFeHCF@rGO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0002">2b</a>) except that micron NaFeHCF grows slowly under hydrothermal conditions without adding iron salts and GO (reaction: Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> +C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> → Na<sub>x</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>). More importantly, the scanning transmission electronic microscopic image (STEM) revealed a precise one-corner-cut cubic morphology, which were indeed different from the traditional NaFeHCF cubics in the previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the meanwhile, the index is exactly the same with the (111) plane of the NaFeHCF, firmly demonstrating the epitaxial nucleation and growth of the nanocrystals on the GO. The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0002">2c</a> elucidate that the distribution of Na, Fe, and O elements is in good agreement with the contour of NaFeHCF crystals, while C element covers the entire crystal and its marginal regions, suggesting that NaFeHCF crystals are anchored solidly on the rGO.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303015-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/349a1769-9b6a-410e-a5cd-ea95a4389f74/aenm202303015-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/349a1769-9b6a-410e-a5cd-ea95a4389f74/aenm202303015-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5594dc70-7cbc-4a5f-b3c7-6f9e529d0cd0/aenm202303015-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/349a1769-9b6a-410e-a5cd-ea95a4389f74/aenm202303015-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303015-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303015" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology characteristics of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO. a) Morphological and structural diagrams of cubic and cornered NaFeHCF. b) The TEM images and c) EDX-elemental mapping images of NaFeHCF@rGO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>HRTEM indicates that NaFeHCF grows on the external surface of rGO and their lattices are coupled to each other, with a crystal plane spacing of ≈0.40&nbsp;nm corresponding to the (220) crystal plane of NaFeHCF, while a crystal plane spacing of ≈ 0.35&nbsp;nm corresponds to the (002) crystal plane of rGO (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern indicates the coexistence of diffraction rings and spots, which are assigned to the (002) reflections of rGO and (220), (422), (822) crystal plane of NaFeHCF, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0003">3c</a>). In addition, Raman imaging is employed to observe the distribution of newly grown layers or structures during epitaxial growth. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0003">3d</a> depicts the macroscopic 3D image of the as-prepared sample, with a region selected for Raman imaging testing. All Raman spectra recorded in this location are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0003">3e</a>. For Raman imaging analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0003">3f</a>), the characteristic peaks of rGO at ≈1340 and ≈1593&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are classified as blue, and the characteristic peaks at ≈2090 and≈2150&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;of NaFeHCF are defined as red. The color intensity indicates the strength of the Raman signal. As described in Figure 3(g–i), there is almost no characteristic peak of rGO in the central red region, and the characteristic peak intensity of NaFeHCF and rGO in the middle green area is equivalent, whereas the characteristic peak intensity of rGO is noticeably stronger than that of NaFeHCF in the outer blue area. This suggests that the NaFeHCF crystal is indeed progressively increasing on the external surface of rGO, forming new layers and structures.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303015-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/695f5421-4b50-49a8-9715-18dfe7977967/aenm202303015-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/695f5421-4b50-49a8-9715-18dfe7977967/aenm202303015-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e7facf87-154e-4d4f-9463-9a5a78a3e5ef/aenm202303015-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/695f5421-4b50-49a8-9715-18dfe7977967/aenm202303015-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303015-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303015" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) HRTEM and c) SAED images of NaFeHCF@rGO. d) The macroscopic 3D image of the as-prepared NaFeHCF@rGO sample and e) all Raman spectra collected in this region. f) The Raman imaging pattern. Representative Raman spectra of g) green, h) red, and i) blue regions.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0080-title">3.2 Crystaline and Chemical Analysis of the Low-Defect NaFeHCF</h3>
            
            <p>XRD patterns expound that bare NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO composite are both indexed to the typical face-centered cubic structure with the space group of Fm<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/51a61b8a-657b-47f2-a24e-54968dcdbde7/aenm202303015-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202303015:aenm202303015-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202303015-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>3</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> m (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>; <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0012_R_d15358767e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Res.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0025_R_d15358767e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the further XRD structure refinement, the lattice parameters of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO samples are assessed to be <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = <i>c</i>&nbsp;= 10.3295&nbsp;A˚ and <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = <i>c</i> = 10.28443&nbsp;A˚ with α = β = γ&nbsp;= 90°, respectively. According to Schiller's formula, the average grain size of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO are calculated to be 18, and 35&nbsp;nm, respectively. Importantly, the relative intensity of the NaFeHCF@rGO diffraction peak is significantly stronger than that of NaFeHCF. The half-peak width of bare NaFeHCF (0.475°) acquired by fitting is much larger than that of NaFeHCF@rGO (0.261°), demonstrating that the GO could dramatically improve the crystallinity of NaFeHCF. Ideally, the arrangement of atoms in the crystal is perfectly ordered, and the diffraction pattern should be very sharp and clear. However, in practice, there are various defects and impurities in the crystal, which will lead to the broadening of the diffraction pattern, that is, the half-peak width increases. Therefore, the crystal's crystallinity increases as half-peak width decreases. Ordinarily, the enhancement of crystallinity indicates that the atomic arrangement in the crystal structure is more regular and the variation range of crystal face spacing and cell size is modest,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0028_R_d15358767e992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - springer berlin">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> confirming that the epitaxial nucleation and growth strategy could effectively decrease the content of the defects in the NaFeHCF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0029_R_d15358767e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0031_R_d15358767e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303015-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bc025fb8-c5e6-49fb-b2aa-7c69c5f7a32f/aenm202303015-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bc025fb8-c5e6-49fb-b2aa-7c69c5f7a32f/aenm202303015-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fa348c67-78d8-42e6-a278-59f2a4aa9f59/aenm202303015-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bc025fb8-c5e6-49fb-b2aa-7c69c5f7a32f/aenm202303015-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303015-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303015" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization of as-prepared NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO samples: a,b) The refined XRD patterns. c) Raman spectrums. d) Fe 2p XPS spectra for NaFeHCF@rGO. e) C1s for GO. f) C1s for NaFeHCF@rGO. g) Diagram of GO converted to rGO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The chemical compositions of the as-prepared NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO are estimated by EA, ICP-OES (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) and TGA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0032_R_d15358767e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0033_R_d15358767e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Commun.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As such, the H<sub>2</sub>O contents in NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO analyzed by TGA are ≈13 wt% within the temperature range of 30 to 280°C. Then, combined with ICP-OES and EA, the molecular formulas of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO could be represented as Na<sub>0.56</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.79</sub>◻<sub>0.21</sub>·1.93H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>1.05</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.87</sub>◻<sub>0.13</sub>·2.16H<sub>2</sub>O (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202303015-tbl-0001">1</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0034_R_d15358767e1064" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. It is found that the Na-content in NaFeHCF@rGO (7.42 wt%) is higher than that of pristine NaFeHCF (4.74 wt%), owing to the reductibility of both rGO and C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> could prevent the oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup>/[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>],<sup>3-[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0012_R_d15358767e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Res.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thus reducing the average valence of Fe and increasing the Na contents in the composite. More significantly, the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> defect content of NaFeHCF@rGO (0.13) is 0.08 per formula unit lower than that of NaFeHCF (0.21), quantificationally referring that our strategy can effectively lattice regularity of the crystals.</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="aenm202303015-tbl-0001">

               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>Further structural and composition information could be harvested from FT-IR (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0004">4c</a>), XPS characterization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0004">4d-f</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), and <sup>57</sup>Fe-Mössbauer spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). First, FT-IR, Raman,XPS and <sup>57</sup>Fe-Mössbauer spectrum evince that Fe's valence state in NaFeHCF is the coexistence of bivalence and trivalence, and more detailed explanation could be found in the supporting information. Importantly, Raman and XPS confirm that GO undergoes a transition to rGO after hydrothermal procedure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0004">4g</a>). The Raman peaks near ≈1355 and 1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the typical D peak (structural defects, edge defects, and dangling bonds) and G peak (in-plane sp<sup>2</sup> carbon) of GO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0035_R_d15358767e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0036_R_d15358767e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Catal. Today">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, authenticating that GO is factually modified on the surface of NaFeHCF crystal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0021_R_d15358767e1239" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, it is worth noting that the peak strength ratios of D peak and G peak in NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO are calculated as 0.97 and 1.02, respectively, implying that GO is transformed into highly conductive rGO after the reaction. Meanwhile, the C 1s core-level XPS spectra of the GO are deconvoluted into five components (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0004">4e</a>): C─C/C═C (285.1&nbsp;eV), C─O (286.3&nbsp;eV), C═O (287.6&nbsp;eV), O─C═O (290.8&nbsp;eV), and the C─O could be designated as C─OH (hydroxyl) or C─O─C (epoxy) functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0037_R_d15358767e1248" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0038_R_d15358767e1248" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the carbon environment of the rGO in NaFeHCF@rGO is divided as: C─C/C═C (285.0&nbsp;eV), C─O (286.3&nbsp;eV), C═O (288.6&nbsp;eV), O─C═O (289.1&nbsp;eV), showing that the C═O peak intensity is weaker than that of GO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0037_R_d15358767e1253" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0038_R_d15358767e1253" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, based on the quantitative analysis of XPS, the O/C mass ratio is significantly reduced from 0.40 in GO to 0.29 in NaFeHCF@rGO, elucidating that a large amount of GO is indeed reduced to highly conductive rGO after hydrothermal treatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0037_R_d15358767e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And it turns out that glucose (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) donates electrons to GO to reduce it to rGO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0090-title">3.3 Unprecedented Electrochemical Performance by the Low-Defect NaFeHCF</h3>
            
            <p>The decreased contents of defects can stabilize the crystalline structure of the NaFeHCF and greatly activate the electrochemical reactions associated with the Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox couple, delivering a prolonged cycling stability and high capacity. As a proof of concept, electrochemical Na-storage performances of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO are characterized by the galvanostatic charge–discharge tests. It is observed that two voltage plateaus are located at higher ≈3.5&nbsp;V and lower ≈2.8&nbsp;V, associated with redox from low-spin Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> couple coordinated with C and high-spin Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> couple connected to N (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0005">5a</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0039_R_d15358767e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The initial charge and discharge capacities of NaFeHCF@rGO are 66.6/124.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are both higher than that of 52.8/110.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for bare NaFeHCF. Accordingly, the electrochemical activity of low-spin Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) at high voltage is extremely inhibited by the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies in the structure as compared to that of the Fe<sup>HS</sup>(N) redox couple. The atoms around the vacancy will lose their redox activity resulting in a loss of capacity. In addition, PBAs have lower electronic conductivity in a fully charged state (i.e., at high voltage), which leads to greater polarization and hinders capacity output.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0040_R_d15358767e1315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Eng. J.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, in this work, the capacity improvement during the 1st charge mainly occurs at high voltage for the following two aspects. The introduced GO can serve as the nucleation and epitaxial growth platform, which greatly improves the lattice regularity of the NaFeHCF, thereby activating the Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox couple and increasing the active sites, resulting in a higher specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e1323" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0016_R_d15358767e1323" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the meanwhile, the increased reversible capacity is also attributable to the enhanced conductivity of the composite material due to the hydrothermal reduction of GO to rGO, and the conductive rGO can slightly improve working voltage by reducing overpotential during the charge/discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0040_R_d15358767e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Eng. J.">40</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0041_R_d15358767e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303015-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c086e473-7496-4d0e-a839-c5383453ec80/aenm202303015-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c086e473-7496-4d0e-a839-c5383453ec80/aenm202303015-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/60acc525-a2ee-48c2-bb12-0cd91fc02fe7/aenm202303015-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c086e473-7496-4d0e-a839-c5383453ec80/aenm202303015-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303015-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303015" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical properties of the NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO: a) The charge–discharge curves at a current density of 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. b) The cycle performances at a current density of 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and c) 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and d) 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. e) The rate capability at current rates ranging from 2&nbsp;C (200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) to 90&nbsp;C (9000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). f) The rate performance of the NaFeHCF@rGO compared with the previously reported PBAs cathodes in NIBs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>At a lower current density of 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the bare NaFeHCF electrode merely retains 63% of its initial capacity after 50 cycles, while the capacity retention of the NaFeHCF@rGO is evidently increased to 85% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0005">5b</a>). Simultaneously, at a higher current density of 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the bare NaFeHCF maintains only 30% of its initial capacity after 500 cycles. Comparatively, the NaFeHCF@rGO exhibits much better cycling stability with capacity retention of 82%, 69%, and 64% after 50, 300, and 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0005">5c</a>), respectively. It should be noted that the NaFeHCF@rGO with lower defects and higher crystallinity could maintain a more stable structure during charge and discharge, thus achieving more outstanding cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e1378" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, it is obvious that the attenuation of capacity is mainly happens in the first 50 cycles, arising from the formation of the cathode-electrolyte-interface (CEI) on nanoparticles with large specific surface area.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0042_R_d15358767e1384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the rGO coating could play the role of protecting the surface of electrode, which is also conducive to the enhancement of cycling stability. Additionally, it should be noted that cycling stability of the electrode is firmly associated with the charge and discharge protocols, especially the cut-off potentials. A set of long cycle studies with NaFeHCF@rGO conducted at a higher current density of 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under the voltage window of 2–3.8&nbsp;V is conducted, as illustrated in figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0005">5d</a> corresponding to a much-improved capacity-retention rate of ≈81% at the 1000th cycle relative to the first cycle.</p>
            
            <p>The rate capabilities of the NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO electrodes are further investigated within the current densities of 200 to 9000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0005">5e</a>, the bare NaFeHCF electrode presents specific capacities of 114.5, 88, 76.4, 59.8, 44.3, and 19.3&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the current rates of 200, 400, 800, 1200, 2000, and 3000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, behaving a rapid capacity decay with the increment of current density. Moreover, only increasing the current density to 4000 or 5000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the NaFeHCF electrode cannot release the capacity (0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). On the contrary, the NaFeHCF@rGO electrode could deliver a remarkable rate capability with capacities of 121.7, 113.4, 111, 108.3, 106.5, 103.3 and 102.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, at the current rates of 200, 400, 800, 1200, 2000, 3000 and 4000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It is worthwhile to emphasize that even if the current density is increased to 5000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the NaFeHCF@rGO electrode exhibited a reversible capacity of 101&nbsp;4 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is 83% of the capacity at 200&nbsp;mAg<sup>−1</sup>. Further increasing the current density to 6000, 7000 and&nbsp;8000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of NaFeHCF@rGO is slightly reduced to 99.3, 98.5, 97.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at the ultra-high rate of 9000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (90&nbsp;C, discharging in 39&nbsp;s), the NaFeHCF@rGO electrode still maintains an exceptionally outstanding capacity of 96.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, equivalent to 80% of its capacity at 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which surpasses most state-of-the-art PBAs cathodes utilized in SIB as well as the lithium-ion battery, as summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0005">5f</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e1436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0022_R_d15358767e1436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0041_R_d15358767e1436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0043_R_d15358767e1436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mater. Chem.">43</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0053_R_d15358767e1436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Mater. Chem. A">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the current density returns to 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of NaFeHCF@rGO electrode could recover to 105.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, we have added a set of rate-performance tests of the reference group NaFeHCF@rGO-R, which was made by mixing NaFeHCF particles with rGO after separate hydrothermal treatment and then physical grinding. The capacity density of NaFeHCF@rGO-R particles at 9&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current density is only ≈54&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that the improved rate performance of NaFeHCF@rGO-R is not simply attributable to the excellent conductivity of rGO. Such exceptional rate capability of NaFeHCF@rGO is due to the synergy of the shorter and superior Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path provided by low-defect NaFeHCF nanoparticals, and the high conductivity network supplied by the tightly integrated rGO coating.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303015-sec-0100-title">3.4 Stable Structural Evolution and Enhanced Cationic Diffusion in Low-Defect NaFeHCF</h3>
            
            <p>To enucleate the structure evolution and redox mechanism during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion-extraction process, in situ XRD and ex situ Raman tests are performed at various states-of-charge (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0006">6</a>). Before cycling, the NaFeHCF@rGO is assigned to the cubic structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0006">6b</a>). Raman spectrum reflects that two weaker peaks at ≈2092 and 2127&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are associated with the C≡N coordinated with bivalent Fe<sup>2+</sup>, and a stronger peak at ≈2155&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the C≡N coordinated with trivalent Fe<sup>3+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303015-fig-0006">6c</a>). In the first charging cycle, the diffraction planes of (200), (220), (400), and (420) shift to higher angles, which means the decrease of lattice parameters with Na<sup>+</sup> released from lattices, and the continuous shift of these peaks strongly suggests a relatively low energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the long periodic framework. And the NaFeHCF@rGO remains as cubic structure. Simultaneously, the υ(CN) Raman peaks move from ≈2099, 2130, and 2155&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> to the higher wavenumbers of ≈2102, 2137, and 2163&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that the remaining Fe<sup>2+</sup> in NaFeHCF@rGO is gradually oxidized to Fe<sup>3+</sup> during charging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0012_R_d15358767e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Res.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0015_R_d15358767e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303015-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/527238d0-3f8b-4356-b4e0-6a5b519425c8/aenm202303015-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/527238d0-3f8b-4356-b4e0-6a5b519425c8/aenm202303015-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4cb7bac9-2077-4963-be9f-235bba8ad40d/aenm202303015-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/527238d0-3f8b-4356-b4e0-6a5b519425c8/aenm202303015-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303015-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303015" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure evolution and electrochemical reaction mechanism of NaFeHCF@rGO. a) Galvanostatic charge/charge voltage profles. b) In situ XRD. c) Ex situ Raman. d) Schematic illustration of the redox mechanism.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In the reverse discharge process, on discharging to 3.2&nbsp;V, new XRD peaks appear at the low angles of 23.8° and 38.4° (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), corresponding to (220) and (422) crystal planes of the rhombohedral phase, respectively. Concurrently, the υ(CN) Raman peaks shift slightly to the lower wavenumbers of ≈2085, 2126, and 2148&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> with the relative peak intensity varying greatly. Further discharging to 2.0&nbsp;V, NaFeHCF@rGO maintains the rhombohedral structure, suggesting that the two-phase transitions from cubic to rhombohedral phase after the first cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e1527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0016_R_d15358767e1527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is due to the Na-deficient NaFeHCF@rGO (0.78Na<sup>+</sup>) electrode is embedded with more Na<sup>+</sup> (1.47Na<sup>+</sup>), reducing the symmetry of crystal structure, leading to the distortion from cubic to rhombometral phase. At this stage, two υ(CN) Raman peaks with equal intensities emerge at ≈2085 and 2136&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that Fe<sup>3+</sup> is progressively reduced to Fe<sup>2+</sup> during the discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e1546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0014_R_d15358767e1546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Similarly, in the second cycle, the XRD pattern converts into a single peak at ≈24, 38° after Na-removal during the charging, with the υ(CN) Raman peaks shift to higher wavenumbers of ≈2097, 2133, and 2156&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. Then the XRD pattern evolve into double peaks after Na-insertion during the discharge process, with the υ(CN) Raman peaks gradually move to lower wavenumbers of ≈2100 and 2134&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. In short, it is demonstrated that NaFeHCF@rGO undergo a reversible redox reaction of two-electron transfer after the second cycle, which could be summarized as: rhombometral Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sup>II</sup>Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub> ↔cubic NaFe<sup>III</sup>Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub> ↔cubic Fe<sup>III</sup>Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>. Noticeably, it is generally considered that the highly reversible structural evolution from the low-defect crystalline also accounts for its decent cyclic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0023_R_d15358767e1579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> XRD studies on NaFeHCF@rGO electrodes before cycling and after 100 cycles at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are performed to further demonstrate the structural stability of NaFeHCF@rGO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) with different sweep rates are conducted to illustrate the kinetic properties of electrode reactions. The Nyquist plots of the NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO present a semicircle in the high-frequency region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), which is associated with the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0054_R_d15358767e1598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Corros. Sci.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The linear slope area in the low-frequency region belongs to the Warburg diffusion process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0055_R_d15358767e1604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Power Sources">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fitted by the equivalent circuit (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11e</a>, Supporting Information), the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of NaFeHCF is dropped from the initial 807.8 to 444.9&nbsp;Ω in 10th cycle, while the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value NaFeHCF@rGO is decreased from the initial 819.4 to 162.9&nbsp;Ω with a larger decline.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0013_R_d15358767e1619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0015_R_d15358767e1619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0018_R_d15358767e1619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0021_R_d15358767e1619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0047_R_d15358767e1619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Electrochem. Commun.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After 20 cycles, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO remain almost unchanged, 427.3 and 164&nbsp;Ω, respectively, indicating that a stable electrochemical reaction state is reached. The smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of NaFeHCF@rGO expounds the faster charge transfer kinetics of the electrode/electrolyte interface, which is advantageous to achieve better rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0045_R_d15358767e1631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - RSC Adv.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In addition, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub>) of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO are also evaluated by cyclic volammetry (CV) with 5.13×10<sup>−11</sup> and 2.55×10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;S<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0056_R_d15358767e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Energy Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, demonstrating a more than 5 times enhancement in the low-defect NaFeHCF. As a result, NaFeHCF@rGO not only shows faster electrochemical reaction kinetics, but also provides higher inside Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability, which could be attributed to the more regular crystal structure and smaller particle size, conducing to improve the diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup>. It's also worth noting that after 50 cycles, the Nyquist curves of NaFeHCF and NaFeHCF@rGO emerge new semicircle in high-frequency area assigned to the film resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>), and the fitted <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> value of NaFeHCF@rGO (112.8&nbsp;Ω) is smaller than that of NaFeHCF (146.9&nbsp;Ω). This is due to the fact that the dense coating of the rGO reduces the surface area of NaFeHCF, thus inhibiting the side reactions between the active electrodes and the electrolytes, and conducing to better cycling performances of the NaFeHCF@rGO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0057_R_d15358767e1669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">57</a>-<a href="#aenm202303015-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303015-bib-0059_R_d15358767e1669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Carbon">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303015-sec-0110">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303015-sec-0110-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>To conclude, we have designed an epitaxial nucleation and growth strategy to manufacture NaFeHCF@rGO materials with low defect, good crystallization, enhanced ionic diffusion, and excellent conductivity. The results demonstrate that, first, GO acts as the nucleation site and guides the subsequent crystallization of NaFeHCF due to the perfect match of their lattice, which improves the structural regularity of the NaFeHCF crystal, thus benefiting the faster Na<sup>+</sup>-ions transmission in the framework while maintaining the structure more stable during the cycle. Moreover, as a dispersant, GO makes the particle size of NaFeHCF smaller and reasonably dispersed, which could provide a shorter Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path and facilitate electrolyte penetration. In addition, GO is reduced to highly conductive rGO in the in situ hydrothermal process, and the close contact between rGO and NaFeHCF could supply speedy e- transport. Furthermore, GO as a protective layer could lessen the side reaction between NaFeHCF nanoparticles and electrolyte. With the synergistic impact of advanced structure and morphology, the cycle and rate performances of the NaFeHCF@rGO are significantly improved. It could release the reversible capacity of 124.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with 76.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> retained after 500 cycles under the high current density of 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (5&nbsp;C), and achieve an extraordinarily superior rate capability of 96.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 9000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (90&nbsp;C), delivering a power density of 23&nbsp;228&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Our discoveries open up new avenues for the creation of regulated crystallization processes as well as realistic sodium batteries with high power densities.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
